The Pitot-Static System, sensitive to airspeed, altitude, and rates of altitude change, provides this pressure information to the Air Data Computer (ADC). The ADC converts this information to a digital signal where it is then electronically sent to, and displayed on, the pilot’s and co-pilot’s Primary Flight Display (PFD). Pilot and Co-Pilot Static Air Pressure Ports – provides references to the air data computer for the pilot’s and co-pilot’s PFD and ESIS for display.  Pilot Alternate Static Air – provides static air to the Pilot and ESIS air data computer in the event that the primary static ports become blocked.  Pilot Static Air Source Control Valve – allows the pilot to switch from NORMAL to ALTERNATE if the primary static ports become blocked. An ISS/LSC pre-warn consists of the following changes:  The ISS/LSC marker is emphasized by changing to a wider red and “red outlined black” checker board pattern.  The Airspeed Digital Readout changes color to yellow and flashes for five seconds, then steady. This boundary is identified by a white line which separates the two.    This horizon line pitches and turns about the airplane symbol, showing aircraft attitude in reference to this line. Pressure Setting  The barometric pressure setting is displayed below the altitude scale on the PFD.  The BARO pressure control knob mounted on the Display Control Panel (DCP) is twisted to set the current altimeter setting.  To set 29.92 when passing FL 180, the inside PUSH STD knob is pushed.  When the pressure settings for the pilot and copilot are different by more than 0.02 In. Hg, a yellow line is drawn beneath each pressure setting and the PSA on the non-coupled side.  At power up, the previous barometric setting is restored but may flash yellow if the current altitude differs from previous altitude by more than 200 ft. Resetting the barometric pressure with the BARO knob stops the flashing.  Radio altitude is shown on the bottom of the attitude display in green and comes into view when the aircraft comes within 2500 feet of the ground. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_1267","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_1267","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"question":{"interactionId":"","quizId":-1,"text":"Image ","type":"graded","interactionType":"choice","score":{"weight":1,"penalty":0}},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_1268","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_1268","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"question":{"interactionId":"","quizId":-1,"text":"Image ","type":"graded","interactionType":"choice","score":{"weight":1,"penalty":0}},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide112017","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"t03p061","thumbnail":"","children":["si145534","si145540","Click_Box_415","Text_Caption_4940","Image_1267","Image_1268","Widget_22"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":90},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide112017"}}},{"id":"si145542","class":"TODO::Senthil","title":"$$uv_Content_Slide_Title$$ ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"si145548","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"question":{"interactionId":"","quizId":-1,"text":"Image ","type":"graded","interactionType":"choice","score":{"weight":1,"penalty":0}},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Click_Box_416","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Click_Box_416","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_4941","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_4941","title":"Barometric Pressure Setting  When the radio altitude comes within 270 feet of the ground, the brown scale (indicating the ground) begins to move up from the bottom of the altimeter and  continues until the aircraft meets the runway at the barometric altitude window.  Decision height from a precision approach is given in feet above the runway. This number can be set into the radio altimeter with the Display Control Panel and is displayed below the altimeter setting.  A blue line to the left of the altimeter shows the pilot where the decision height is in relation to the ground on the bottom of the altimeter. When this blue line moves up to become level with the barometric altitude box, the pilot has reached decision height. Level 180 Alert  The flight level 180 alert reminds the pilots that they must reset their altimeter to 29.92 when climbing through 18000 feet MSL. Another Altimeter Reset reminder occurs when descending through 18000 feet.  The alert causes the barometric pressure setting to start flashing.  In a climb, the alert triggers when the aircraft is 200 feet below 18000 feet MSL (flight level 180) and the altimeter has not been set to 29.92.  In a descent, the alert triggers when 500 feet above flight level 180 and the altimeter is still set at 29.92.  To disable this alert, the BARO knob on the DCP is pushed or turned, or by using the altitude knob on the flight guidance panel. The RA MIN and the BARO MIN function allows the pilot or copilot to set DH (Decision Height) or MDA (Minimum Descent Altitude) for an approach.  To change any of these numbers, the adjacent Line Select Key is selected to box the number. Once the number is boxed, it starts to flash indicating that the MENU ADV knob can be turned to change the value. A clockwise rotation increases the number while a counterclockwise rotation decreases the number. This is used so radar returns can be better analyzed.   Normally, receiver gain is set to 0 or NORM. Other settings are -3, -2, -1, 1, 2, or 3. Minus numbers reduce the sensitivity by approximately one color level. This results in a total reduction of three color levels at -3. Therefore, if a target was magenta in NORM, the same target at -3 would almost be undetectable or be barely seen.    Because of this, the WX radar should not be operated in one of these reduced gain settings for an extended period of time. The ESIS includes an internal ADC that connects to the pilot’s Pitot-Static System and provides barometric corrected altitude, airspeed, and vertical speed information. Because of the lead/lag factor during turns, actual heading for turns through north will lead what is currently being displayed on the compass and the pilot must roll out early or prior to the heading desired on the magnetic compass. For turns through south, the actual heading lags behind what is currently being displayed on the magnetic compass and the pilot must turn through the desired heading or roll out past the desired heading on the magnetic compass.  For accurate magnetic compass headings the windshield wipers, the windshield anti-ice, air conditioner, electric heat, and vent blower must be off.
